    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="fd5dfbb5944e4ec7bf58e0bb8e411edd">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'b909970a-8d4e-4241-bd64-50e0b1376b60'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

                     <p>E-mail: <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:linyuxiaochris@126.com"><span>linyuxiaochris@126.com</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:xlfan@zju.edu.cn"><span>xlfan@zju.edu.cn</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:libh@sz.tsinghua.edu.cn"><span>libh@sz.tsinghua.edu.cn</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:guohchen@cityu.edu.hk"><span>guohchen@cityu.edu.hk</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:xiaoliang.yu@polyu.edu.hk"><span>xiaoliang.yu@polyu.edu.hk</span></a></p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Chen/Guohua">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Yu/Xiaoliang" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a16" data-db-target-for="a16" aria-controls="a16" aria-haspopup="true" id="a16_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Xiaoliang Yu<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a16" aria-labelledby="a16_Ctrl" role="region" id="a16">
                     <p class="author-type mb-1">Corresponding Author</p>
                     <p class="author-name">Xiaoliang Yu</p>
                     <ul class="rlist sm-account">

                     </ul>
                     <p>Department of Mechanical Engineering and Research Institute for Smart Energy, The Hong Kong Polytechnic University, Hong Kong, 999077 China</p>
                     <p>E-mail: <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:linyuxiaochris@126.com"><span>linyuxiaochris@126.com</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:xlfan@zju.edu.cn"><span>xlfan@zju.edu.cn</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:libh@sz.tsinghua.edu.cn"><span>libh@sz.tsinghua.edu.cn</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:guohchen@cityu.edu.hk"><span>guohchen@cityu.edu.hk</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:xiaoliang.yu@polyu.edu.hk"><span>xiaoliang.yu@polyu.edu.hk</span></a></p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Yu/Xiaoliang">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span></div>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d40189291" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Developing rechargeable batteries with high power delivery at low temperatures (LT) below 0&nbsp;°C is significant for cold-climate applications. Initial anode-free sodium metal batteries (AFSMBs) promise high LT performances because of the low de-solvation energy and smaller Stokes radius of Na<sup>+</sup>, nondiffusion-limited plating/stripping electrochemistry, and maximized energy density. However, the severe reduction in electrolyte ionic conductivity and formation of unstable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) hinder their practical applications at LT. In this study, a 2-methyltetrahydrofuran-based dilute electrolyte is designed to concurrently achieve an anion-coordinated solvation structure and impressive ionic conductivity of 3.58&nbsp;mS&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> at −40&nbsp;°C. The dominant aggregate solvates enable the formation of highly efficient and LT-resistant Na<sup>+</sup> hopping channels in the electrolyte. Moreover, the methyl-regulated electronic structure in 2-methyltetrahydrofuran induces gradient decomposition toward an inorganic-organic bilayer SEI with high Na<sup>+</sup> mobility, composition homogeneity, and mechanical robustness. As such, a record-high Coulombic efficiency beyond 99.9% is achieved even at −40&nbsp;°C. The as-constructed AFSMBs sustain 300 cycles with 80% capacity maintained, and a 0.5-Ah level pouch cell delivers 85% capacity over 180 cycles at −25&nbsp;°C. This study affords new insights into electrolyte formulation for fast ionic conduction and superior Na reversibility at ultralow temperatures.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202408161-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202408161-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Li-ion batteries (LIBs) dominate the global energy storage market for portable electronics and electric vehicles because of their high energy/power density, long cycle life, and environmental friendliness.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0001_R_d40189270e670" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Mater. Nat. Commun.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, severe performance decay occurs at low temperatures (LT) below 0&nbsp;°C, representing a significant obstacle to their deployment in cold-climate applications, including aeronautics/space missions, polar expeditions, and various military and civil facilities.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0002_R_d40189270e675" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The obvious energy and power loss at LT comes from the abruptly reduced electrolyte ionic conductivity, retarded interfacial charge transfer kinetics, and decelerated ion transport through the electrode-electrolyte interfaces and bulk electrodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0003_R_d40189270e680" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Environ. Sci. eTransportation ACS Energy Lett.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Current LIB-powered devices mostly employ supplementary warming systems to guarantee optimal operation at LT, which inevitably reduces the energy density.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0004_R_d40189270e686" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nature Energ. Convers. Manage.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Developing novel energy storage systems with high power output at LT is highly desirable.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0005_R_d40189270e691" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nat. Energy Nat. Energy">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Rechargeable sodium batteries are emerging beyond-Li-ion battery technologies, in which the lower de-solvation energy and smaller Stokes radius of Na<sup>+</sup> provide more opportunities for achieving boosted LT performances.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0006_R_d40189270e700" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Chem. Soc. Rev. ACS Energy Lett.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among various Na battery systems, initial anode-free sodium metal batteries (AFSMBs) coupling a bare anode current collector with a Na-containing cathode minimize the total electrode mass, thus enabling maximized energy densities comparable to LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0007_R_d40189270e705" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - iScience">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The anode electrochemistry of Na plating/stripping is nondiffusion-limited,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0008_R_d40189270e711" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Chem">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> different from conventional diffusion-controlled intercalation,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0009_R_d40189270e716" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Energy Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> alloying,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0010_R_d40189270e721" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Small">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and conversion-based.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0011_R_d40189270e727" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> electrochemistry, and favors further accelerated charge transfer kinetics at LT. Accordingly, AFSMBs are regarded among the most promising candidates for LT batteries with high energy output. Nevertheless, the severe ionic conductivity decrease at LT.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0012_R_d40189270e732" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Mater. Chem. A">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the formation of unstable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0013_R_d40189270e737" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nat. Commun.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> cause uneven Na deposition. The Na plating/stripping Coulombic efficiency (CE) thus falls far below the 99.9+% required for sustainable AFSMB operation. As a result, the LT performances of most state-of-the-art AFSMBs remain impractical.</p>
         
         <p>The electrolyte solvation structure is of critical importance in determining the LT ion conducting property and anodic interfacial reactions. Typically, the solvent-separated ion pair (SSIP) solvate exhibits a robust Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent coordination. The solvation shell remains essentially intact, allowing rapid movement of the Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent complex in the electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0014_R_d40189270e749" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Comput. Chem.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, such smooth ion-conducting channels are sensitive to LT, with solvated Na<sup>+</sup> transport severely retarded by the strong intermolecular attraction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0015_R_d40189270e757" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Nano">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The de-solvation kinetics are also impeded at LT, encouraging a greater number of solvent molecules to participate in the formation of organic-rich SEI.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0016_R_d40189270e762" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Energy Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Contact-ion pair (CIP) and aggregate (AGG) solvates involve anions in the solvation complex, which could reduce the de-solvation energy and induce anion-derived inorganic-rich species in electrode/electrolyte interphases.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0017_R_d40189270e767" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater. Nano Lett. P. Natl. Acad. Sci.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Unfortunately, CIP/AGG-dominated electrolytes typically show sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> conduction. For instance, salt-concentrated electrolytes featuring percolated nanometric aggregates conduct Na<sup>+</sup> ions mainly through the hopping mechanism, yet the high viscosity limits the Na<sup>+</sup>-ion conductivity at LT.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0018_R_d40189270e779" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Energy Lett. Aggregate">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nonsolvating diluents, such as fluorinated ethers, disintegrate bulk AGG clusters into smaller AGGs and reduce/increase the viscosity/wettability, but the ion-conducting efficiency in the electrolytes is suboptimal.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0019_R_d40189270e785" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nano Energy Rare Met.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Their hyperreactivity with Na metal also leads to low interfacial compatibility. Recently proposed weakly solvating electrolytes can facilitate fast desolvation and anion-tuned SEI for rapid Na<sup>+</sup> migration. But the low sodium-salt dissociation impedes rapid Na<sup>+</sup> transport in the electrolyte;<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0013_R_d40189270e794" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nat. Commun.">13</a>, <a href="#adma202408161-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0020_R_d40189270e794" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To date, concurrently achieving an anion-coordinated solvation structure and a high LT ionic conductivity is extremely challenging.</p>
         
         <p>Recently, Fan and colleagues reported a groundbreaking work showcasing the great potential of small-sized fluoroacetonitrile solvent-based electrolytes with hybrid vehicular and structural ion transport (1:1) for achieving superior ionic conductivities.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0021_R_d40189270e802" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nature">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The impact of cooling on the evolution of ion conduction mechanisms has not yet been clarified. In this work, a 2-methyltetrahydrofuran (MeTHF)-based dilute electrolyte with a weak dissociation of Na<sup>+</sup>-PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> ion pairs is developed to build highly reversible AFSMBs at LT. It breaks through the intermolecular attraction-induced LT ion-conducting limitation of conventional electrolytes. The LT-resistant ion conduction channels enable an impressively high ionic conductivity of 3.58&nbsp;mS&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> at −40&nbsp;°C. Furthermore, the small AGG-dominated solvation structure with methyl-regulated electronic structure induces a gradient electrolyte decomposition into an inorganic-organic bilayer SEI with fast Na<sup>+</sup> transfer kinetics, composition homogeneity, and mechanical robustness. As a result, a record-high CE beyond 99.9% is achieved at an ultralow temperature of −40&nbsp;°C, and an AFSMB sustains 300 cycles with an 80% capacity retention. A 0.5-Ah level pouch-type AFSMB delivers a lifespan of over 180 cycles with an 85% capacity retention at −25&nbsp;°C, which is superior to most published results.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202408161-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202408161-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202408161-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202408161-sec-0030-title">2.1 Electrolyte Screening for Highly Reversible AFSMBs at LT</h3>
            
            <p>The Na plating/stripping reversibility at LT is highly related to electrolytes’ freezing properties, ion-pairing features, and decomposition pathways on Na anodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0013_R_d40189270e831" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nat. Commun.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> We first screened Na salts by balancing the ion-pairing level and solubility property (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202408161-fig-0001">1a</a>, left). As summarized in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), sodium tetrafluoroborate (NaBF<sub>4</sub>) exhibits the highest lattice energy, resulting in significant difficulty in salt solubility in the conventional cyclic ether such as MeTHF. Sodium triflate (NaOTf) and sodium hexafluorophosphate (NaPF<sub>6</sub>) have similarly high lattice energies but distinctive solubility properties. NaOTf was slightly dissolved by MeTHF, while NaPF<sub>6</sub> shows a high solubility of &gt;1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span>, owing to the more favorable charge distribution of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> for easy dissolution. Sodium bis(fluorosulfonyl)imide (NaFSI) and sodium bis(trifluoromethylsulfonyl)imide (NaTFSI) with low lattice energies can be well dissolved in MeTHF. Yet, they were reported to show much poorer Na anode compatibility than NaPF<sub>6</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0022_R_d40189270e860" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Environ Sci ACS Cent. Sci.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> as confirmed by the Na reversibility tests (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). As such, NaPF<sub>6</sub> is selected as the main salt in the electrolytes.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202408161-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9ee0e1bb-df0a-49d7-adce-0d2af9d26516/adma202408161-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/9ee0e1bb-df0a-49d7-adce-0d2af9d26516/adma202408161-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/641b3b48-d82d-44f1-8f99-9c5e9b9c864d/adma202408161-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9ee0e1bb-df0a-49d7-adce-0d2af9d26516/adma202408161-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202408161-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202408161" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrolyte screening for high Na reversibility at LT. a) Design principles of LT electrolytes for AFSMBs. b) Cycling stability of Na||Al half cells using MeTHF and G2/DOL electrolytes at 0.5&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>/1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> under −25&nbsp;°C. c) Comparison of LT Na plating/stripping CEs in the MeTHF electrolyte with those reported electrolytes.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0013" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0013_R_d40189270e894" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nat. Commun.">13</a>, <a href="#adma202408161-bib-0025" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0025_R_d40189270e894" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Energy Storage Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. J. Mater. Chem. A Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Joule">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> d) Long-term cycling performance of anode-free coin cells at 0.5&nbsp;C under −25 and −40&nbsp;°C. e) Cyclability of the anode-free pouch cell at −25&nbsp;°C. The inset is the optical image and configuration of the as-fabricated pouch cell.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Cyclic ethers with low melting points, weak solvating power, and high Na metal compatibility were then selected for formulating proper LT electrolytes. Three primary indicators of a solvent's capacity to dissolve salts include Gutmann donor number (<i>DN</i>), dielectric constant (<i>ɛ</i>), and dipole moment (<i>µ</i>),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0023_R_d40189270e908" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Soc. Rev. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> but their individual effects are ambiguous. As summarized in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), there appears to be no discernible correlation of the solvent's NaPF<sub>6</sub> solubility with <i>DN</i> but a significant positive correlation with <i>ɛ</i>, except for 1,3-dioxolane (DOL). Upon careful consideration of the indicator <i>µ</i>, it can be observed that high NaPF<sub>6</sub> solubility demonstrates a strong correlation with both high values of <i>ɛ</i> and <i>µ</i> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408161-fig-0001">1a</a>, middle). The electrolyte was thus formulated by dissolving NaPF<sub>6</sub> into MeTHF for investigation (denoted as MeTHF electrolyte), which may own high ionic conductivity, anion-coordinated solvation structure, and low-temperature resistance (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408161-fig-0001">1a</a>, right). A concentration of 0.8&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> was determined because of its optimized electrochemical performance (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). A strongly solvating electrolyte of 0.8&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> dissolving in a mixture of diethylene glycol dimethyl ether (G2) and anti-freezing 1,3-dioxolane (DOL) (≈7:3 in volume and ≈1.1:1 in mole) was prepared as the reference (denoted as G2/DOL electrolyte). Both electrolytes are anti-freezing, which is a prerequisite for LT battery applications (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>The Na plating/stripping reversibility was investigated using Na||Al half cells. At room temperature (RT), the MeTHF electrolyte displays a high and stable Na plating/stripping CE of 99.8% at 0.5&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> under a capacity of 1&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>, close to that of the G2/DOL electrolyte (99.9%) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). The G2/DOL electrolyte shows a gradually increasing CE until stabilization at a high level (≈99.8%) at −25&nbsp;°C, which however, fluctuated severely after 100 plating/stripping cycles and finally caused cell failure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408161-fig-0001">1b</a>, top). In stark contrast, the MeTHF electrolyte demonstrates high and stable CE of beyond 99.9% only after 10 cycles, which maintains quite stable over prolonged 400 cycles. The corresponding voltage profiles do not exhibit any discernible difference over cycling (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). When the current density increases to 1.0&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>, the CE value is still over 99.9% at −25&nbsp;°C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>To further evaluate the LT operation capability of the MeTHF electrolyte, the Na reversibility at the temperature of −40&nbsp;°C was investigated. The stable Na plating/stripping CE values at 0.5&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information) and 1.0&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408161-fig-0001">1b</a>, bottom) were determined to be beyond 99.9% over 200 cycles. The Na plating/stripping reversibility was also estimated by the well-known Aurbach method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0024_R_d40189270e988" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - ACS Mater. Lett.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The MeTHF electrolyte enables a CE beyond 99.9% at both −25 and −40&nbsp;°C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). Such remarkable performance is far superior to state-of-the-art LT electrolytes, as summarized in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408161-fig-0001">1c</a>, including those THF/DOL-based weakly solvating electrolytes, especially considering the applied ultralow temperature of −40&nbsp;°C.</p>
            
            <p>Encouraged by the superior Na reversibility, we constructed AFSMB cells comprising a Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10–S12</a>, Supporting Information) and a carbon-coated aluminum anode current collector. Galvanostatic cycling demonstrates a high initial discharge capacity of 94&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at RT. This value decreases by 6%, 36%, and 57% when the operation temperature is sequentially reduced to 0, −25, and −40&nbsp;°C, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13a</a>, Supporting Information). The CE value increases with reduced temperature, reaching 99.9% at −40&nbsp;°C, indicating the superior reversibility of anode-free cells. Moreover, the capacity is well recovered as the temperature returns to room temperature. The charge–discharge curves show negligible voltage polarization and little depression with temperature stepping (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13b</a>, Supporting Information), suggesting superior charge transfer kinetics. The long-term cycling stability of AFSMBs at LT was investigated with coin-type cells first. The anode-free cell with the MeTHF electrolyte delivers an initial discharge capacity of 82&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and maintains 80% of the capacity over 300 cycles at −25&nbsp;°C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408161-fig-0001">1d</a>). In contrast, the G2/DOL system shows a much lower initial capacity of 57.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, which retains only 75% over 300 cycles. When the temperature is further reduced to −40&nbsp;°C, the G2/DOL system fails to operate (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information); in sharp contrast, the MeTHF system can still stably run for 300 cycles with a high capacity retention of 78%.</p>
            
            <p>To validate the feasibility of the MeTHF electrolyte in practical conditions, a 0.5-Ah-level pouch-type AFSMB was fabricated and tested using double-side-coated Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode films with high areal mass loading of ≈30&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. The obtained charge-discharge voltage profiles at various current rates under −25&nbsp;°C are illustrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> (Supporting Information). As the current rates increase from 0.1, 0.2 to 0.5 C, slight depression of the voltage plateaus can be observed, and high discharge capacities of 0.45, 0.42, and 0.38 Ah are delivered, respectively. The long-term cycling stability of the pouch cell at −25&nbsp;°C is depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408161-fig-0001">1e</a>. It maintains 95.5% of the initial capacity after cycling for 100 cycles at 0.2 C. It remains as high as 85% capacity at a higher discharge rate of 0.5 C over the subsequent 80 cycles. Despite the need for optimization of cell fabrication parameters such as electrolyte amount in this research, such remarkable results have showcased the great potential of the MeTHF electrolyte for practical LT AFSMBs. The power delivery capability at LT of anode-free cells was further estimated based on the total mass of electrodes and current collectors. An LT of −25&nbsp;°C was utilized for the pouch cell test, which can meet the needs of most LT application scenarios. As summarized in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information), the calculated energy density (219&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>) of the as-fabricated anode-free cell is over twice as large as that of the commercial LiFePO<sub>4</sub>||graphite cell (100&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>) at −25&nbsp;°C, revealing its superiority in LT energy output.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202408161-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202408161-sec-0040-title">2.2 Aggregate-Dominated Solvation Structure</h3>
            
            <p>The solvation structures of electrolytes were studied using Raman spectroscopy and density functional theoretical (DFT) calculations. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202408161-fig-0002">2a</a> displays the Raman spectra of investigated electrolytes and their components. A prominent fingerprint peak centered at 850&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> can be detected in the G2 solvent, corresponding to the C−O stretching vibration. This characteristic peak was largely blueshifted by ≈10&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> in the G2/DOL electrolyte, indicating the strong coordination between the G2 solvent and Na<sup>+</sup>. In contrast, the corresponding Na<sup>+</sup> coordination-derived blue shifts of MeTHF are much less noticeable owing to its weak binding with Na<sup>+</sup>, as confirmed by lower binding energies of various Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent complexes revealed by the DFT calculation (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202408161-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8ee72e1a-6b11-457b-bd11-de0cc80f3b08/adma202408161-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/8ee72e1a-6b11-457b-bd11-de0cc80f3b08/adma202408161-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b18ee9b6-25f0-488d-9147-f5ef99373e00/adma202408161-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8ee72e1a-6b11-457b-bd11-de0cc80f3b08/adma202408161-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202408161-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202408161" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Experimental and theoretical analysis of electrolyte solvent structure. a) Raman spectra of the MeTHF and G2/DOL electrolytes and their components. b) Fitted Raman spectra of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions for the MeTHF and G2/DOL electrolytes. Raman peaks from SSIP, CIP, and AGG solvates are located at 738.6, 741.7, and 743.8&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Snapshots obtained from classical molecular dynamics simulations for the MeTHF (c) and G2/DOL (d) electrolytes. Na<sup>+</sup> radial distribution function (RDF) and coordination number in the MeTHF (e) and G2/DOL (f) electrolytes.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The peak at 765&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> is ascribed to the P–F symmetric stretch in NaPF<sub>6</sub>, which shows a prominent red shift in three electrolytes due to largely reduced PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>-Na<sup>+</sup> coordination after dissociation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0026_R_d40189270e1135" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Electrochim. Acta">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A slight blueshift of this peak is observed from the G2/DOL electrolyte compared to the MeTHF electrolyte, indicating the existence of more PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>-Na<sup>+</sup> pairs. Based on the fitting results of the Raman spectra in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408161-fig-0002">2b</a>, the G2/DOL electrolyte shows an SSIP-dominated solvation structure (50.61% SSIP), while AGGs are dominant in the MeTHF electrolyte with a high content of 67.88%. Note that the SSIP proportion is determined to be less than 6%, which has rarely been reported in dilute electrolytes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0018_R_d40189270e1149" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Energy Lett. Aggregate">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Importantly, the anion attributes greatly affect the ion-pairing level in the electrolyte solvates. As evidenced in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information), when NaFSI or NaTFSI salt with much lower lattice energies is used, the SSIP ratio of the as-formulated electrolytes remarkably increases, but the AGG ratio decreases.</p>
            
            <p>Molecular dynamics (MD) simulations were carried out to obtain a more detailed investigation of the solvation structures and Na<sup>+</sup> coordination environments. Many heterogeneously distributed and small-sized anion-cation-solvent clusters are observed in the MeTHF electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408161-fig-0002">2c</a>) owing to the low solvation ability of MeTHF and the weak dissociation of Na<sup>+</sup>-PF<sub>6</sub><sup>–</sup> ion pairs. In contrast, the G2/DOL electrolyte shows a typical SSIP-dominant structure, with most anions uninvolved in the solvates (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408161-fig-0002">2d</a>). The radial distribution functions (RDF) and coordination numbers analysis confirm the significant involvement of anions in the primary solvation sheath of the MeTHF electrolyte and of solvents in that of the G2/DOL electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408161-fig-0002">2e,f</a>), respectively. As the simulation temperature is reduced to an LT of 250&nbsp;K, the small AGG-dominated solvation structure is maintained (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18–S20</a>, Supporting Information). And the theoretically quantified SSIP, CIP, and AGG content (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information) agrees well with the Raman analysis. Note that the proportion of SSIP in the MeTHF electrolyte is negligibly small and thus is of minor consideration in the following discussions.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202408161-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202408161-sec-0050-title">2.3 Low-Temperature-Resistant Ion-Conducting Channels</h3>
            
            <p>A weak salt dissociation in electrolytes reduces the effective charge carrier density and ion-conducting capability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0027_R_d40189270e1195" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Phys. Rev. B J. Electrochem. Soc. Adv. Fun. Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> We thus measured the ionic conductivities of the MeTHF and G2/DOL electrolytes at various temperatures. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202408161-fig-0003">3a</a>, the weakly solvating MeTHF electrolyte exhibits an impressively high ionic conductivity of 5.53&nbsp;mS&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> at RT, comparable to 6.25&nbsp;mS&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> of the G2/DOL electrolyte. More importantly, the ionic conductivity maintains 3.58&nbsp;mS&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> even at −40&nbsp;°C, which is as high as 65% of the RT value. It is far superior to the G2/DOL and other reported MeTHF-based weakly solvating electrolytes,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0020_R_d40189270e1213" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">20</a>, <a href="#adma202408161-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0028_R_d40189270e1213" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Mater. J. Colloid Interf. Sci.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> showcasing its excellence in LT ionic conduction. We determine the transference number (<i>t</i><sub>+</sub>) from the polarization curves (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information). The MeTHF electrolyte shows a much higher <i>t</i><sub>+</sub> value of 0.632 than the G2/DOL electrolyte (0.285). It suggests its high efficiency in conducting positive charge carriers, which is quite favorable for boosting the LT performance of AFSMBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0029_R_d40189270e1228" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202408161-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4a430861-2b44-4ae3-8187-9198f8e73f01/adma202408161-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/4a430861-2b44-4ae3-8187-9198f8e73f01/adma202408161-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/52c6cc0a-b0b5-45af-9ba7-1b2766394834/adma202408161-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4a430861-2b44-4ae3-8187-9198f8e73f01/adma202408161-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202408161-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202408161" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Physicochemical properties of investigated electrolytes. a) Temperature-dependent ionic conductivity of the MeTHF, G2/DOL, and other reported weakly solvating electrolytes. b)Temperature-dependent viscosity of the MeTHF and G2/DOL electrolytes. c) The mean-squared displacements of Na<sup>+</sup> in the MeTHF electrolyte at 25 and −25&nbsp;°C. Evolution of Na-ion solvation environment in the MeTHF (d,e) and G2/DOL (f,g) electrolytes from molecular dynamics simulations at 300 and 250&nbsp;K (the vertical axis represents the serial number of different solvents or anions coordinated with the investigated Na<sup>+</sup> in the simulation time of 2000&nbsp;ps). h) Variation of energy barrier of Na<sup>+</sup> hopping in the MeTHF and G2/DOL electrolytes. Insets, spatial distribution function of Na<sup>+</sup> ions around the MeTHF and G2 solvents. i) Shematic illustrations of ion conducting channels in the MeTHF and G2/DOL electrolytes at RT and LT.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Generally, the electrolyte viscosity derived from the intermolecular interaction of solvents impedes the charge carrier transfer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0030_R_d40189270e1263" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - ACS Energy Lett.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the viscosity–temperature curve (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408161-fig-0003">3b</a>), the G2/DOL electrolyte shows a higher room-temperature viscosity and a much more rapid increase with decreasing temperature than the MeTHF electrolyte. It can be ascribed to the small surface area of the cyclic ether MeTHF toward reduced dispersive forces.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0031_R_d40189270e1271" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The charge carrier mobilities (or diffusion coefficients) of investigated electrolytes at different temperatures were stimulated by tracking the mean-squared displacements (MSD) as a function of the time interval for 970&nbsp;ps. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408161-fig-0003">3c</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a> (Supporting Information), the MeTHF electrolyte owns a much higher room-temperature Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of 4.65&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−10</sup>&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> than G2/DOL (2.83&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−10</sup>&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>). It remains 2.53&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−10</sup>&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> at −25&nbsp;°C, which is over three times as high as the G2/DOL electrolyte (0.71&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−10</sup>&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>).</p>
            
            <p>To understand the fast ion conducting mechanism, we performed MD simulations to investigate the molecular scale solvation dynamics and the anion/solvent residence time around the Na<sup>+</sup> cations, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408161-fig-0003">3d–g</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24–S26</a> (Supporting Information). The residence time of Na<sup>+</sup> in the MeTHF electrolyte is remarkably short, indicating frequent solvation of Na<sup>+</sup> by MeTHF molecules (≈134&nbsp;ps at 300&nbsp;K) and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions (≈332&nbsp;ps at 300&nbsp;K) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408161-fig-0003">3d</a>). The highly rapid Na<sup>+</sup> hopping frequency between the neighboring coordination sites results in high ionic conductivity. Moreover, such Na<sup>+</sup>-ion solvation environment does not significantly change as the temperature is reduced to 250&nbsp;K, with slightly extended coordination periods of ≈203&nbsp;ps for MeTHF molecules and 390&nbsp;ps for PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408161-fig-0003">3e</a>). In the G2/DOL electrolyte, G2 dominates the Na<sup>+</sup> solvation sheath because of its strong affinity to Na<sup>+</sup>. Na<sup>+</sup> transfers through the vehicular mechanism, in which Na<sup>+</sup> is carried along by the solvation sheaths with a longer coordination period of about 490.8&nbsp;ps at 300&nbsp;K (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408161-fig-0003">3f</a>). Owing to the increasing viscosity, the residence time is extended to 1336.7&nbsp;ps as the temperature is reduced to 250&nbsp;K (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408161-fig-0003">3g</a>). When the temperature is further reduced to 233&nbsp;K, the Na<sup>+</sup>-ion solvation environment still does not show an enormous change (see Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a>, Supporting Information), indicating the good LT resistance of ion-conducting channels in our MeTHF electrolyte.</p>
            
            <p>The energetic Na<sup>+</sup> hopping in the MeTHF electrolyte is also verified by the decreased activation energy of 0.48&nbsp;eV compared to 1.08&nbsp;eV of the G2/DOL electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408161-fig-0003">3h</a>). Recall that the ratio of AGG/CIP solvates in the MeTHF electrolyte is ≈3:1. The CIP solvates are in small sizes with low steric hindrance to migration. Therefore, they can serve as efficient intermediates for Na<sup>+</sup> hopping between adjacent AGG solvates (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408161-fig-0003">3i</a>). Such ion-conducting channels are preserved at LT for several reasons. First, the solvation structure of the MeTHF electrolyte is well maintained at LT. Second, the solvation/desolvation of Na<sup>+</sup> ions can easily occur at LT, enabling smooth Na<sup>+</sup> exchange between neighboring AGG and CIP solvates. Third, the increase in intermolecular attractions among solvates is negligible at reduced temperatures. In stark contrast, despite the rapid Na<sup>+</sup> conduction in the G2/DOL electrolyte at RT, the ion-conducting pathways are mostly blocked owing to the prominent intermolecular interactions. It has been demonstrated that the presence of anions in the solvation sheaths promotes frequent hopping of cations.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0021_R_d40189270e1384" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nature">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Our results further highlight the necessity of maintaining an AGG-dominated solvation structure to achieve superior LT ionic conductivity. The observed rapid charge carrier transfer mechanism, primarily driven by AGG hopping, is substantiated by a notable reduction in ionic conductivity from 5.53 to 1.95&nbsp;mS&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> as the salt concentration of NaPF<sub>6</sub>-MeTHF electrolytes decreases from 0.8 to 0.5&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span>. The AGG content is significantly reduced from 68% to 27%, leading to a substantial decrease in the number of effective charge carriers and breaking of the ion-conducting network (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27–S29</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202408161-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202408161-sec-0060-title">2.4 Mechanistic Understanding of Superior Na Reversibility</h3>
            
            <p>The composition and microstructure of SEI could largely affect the interfacial stability and the Na plating/stripping reversibility.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0032_R_d40189270e1408" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Comprehensive and precise analysis of the SEI formation pathway, particularly the reduction sequence of electrolyte components at the Na anode interface, is critical for understanding the SEI structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0033_R_d40189270e1413" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Accordingly, DFT calculations were performed to evaluate their lowest unoccupied molecular orbital (LUMO) energy levels and Fukui functions. We found that the energy levels of free anions and solvents are much reduced after solvation (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). As illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202408161-fig-0004">4a</a>, the small AGG solvate of 2Na<sup>+</sup>−1PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>-4MeTHF and the SSIP solvate of Na<sup>+</sup>-MeTHF own the lowest LUMO energy levels of −1.42 and −1.43&nbsp;eV, respectively, predicting their preferential decomposition. The Fukui functions of the 2Na<sup>+</sup>−1PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>-4MeTHF solvate in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30</a> (Supporting Information) provide further insight into the electron density distributions around investigated atoms. The higher values around P and F atoms suggest higher electron density, thus favoring anion reduction to inorganic SEI species.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0034_R_d40189270e1444" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Nat. Energy">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Considering the low SSIP proportion in the MeTHF electrolyte, we deduced that anion decomposition dominates its SEI formation process. Note that the methyl functional group plays a critical role in regulating the electronic structure of the solvent molecules and solvation complexes, thus changing the SEI formation pathway. In the case of the 0.8&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in THF electrolyte, the SSIP solvate of Na<sup>+</sup>-THF exhibits a much lower LUMO energy level of −1.43 than −0.49&nbsp;eV of the CIP solvate (1Na<sup>+</sup>−1PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>-3THF) and 0.03&nbsp;eV of the small AGG solvate (1Na<sup>+</sup>−2PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>-2THF) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S31</a>, Supporting Information), suggesting its preferential reduction to organic SEI species. This decomposition pathway is quite different from the MeTHF counterpart despite their similar small-AGG-dominated solvation structures (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S32–S36</a>, Supporting Information). In the case of the G2/DOL electrolyte, the energy level gap between the SSIP solvate (Na<sup>+</sup>−2G2, −0.65&nbsp;eV) and CIP solvate (Na<sup>+</sup>-PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>-G2, −0.01&nbsp;eV) is not that prominent, allowing the simultaneous decomposition of solvents and anions to form an organic-inorganic hybrid structure.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202408161-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/980568f7-e5e7-459a-976b-46fdb3e947ef/adma202408161-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/980568f7-e5e7-459a-976b-46fdb3e947ef/adma202408161-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3a491991-73cd-472b-87f2-8f40eb47a746/adma202408161-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/980568f7-e5e7-459a-976b-46fdb3e947ef/adma202408161-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202408161-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202408161" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Formation pathway and structural characterization of SEI. a) LUMO energy levels of solvation complexes of interest in the MeTHF and G2/DOL electrolytes. 3D rendering for the C<sub>2</sub>HO<sup>‒</sup>, NaF<sup>−</sup> secondary ions and the overlay in the ToF-SIMS tested for the SEI formed in the MeTHF (b) and G2/DOL (c) electrolytes at −25&nbsp;°C.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To realize 3D characterization and structural reconstruction of the SEI formed at LT, we carried out depth profile analyses of time-of-flight secondary ion mass spectrometry (ToF-SIMS). The NaF<sup>−</sup> and C<sub>2</sub>HO<sup>−</sup> signals represent characteristic ionic fragments of NaF and organic components in SEI, respectively. We find that abundant C<sub>2</sub>HO<sup>−</sup> species exist at the surface of SEI formed in the MeTHF electrolyte (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S37a</a>, Supporting Information), which witnessed a fast reduction with sputtering. In contrast, the content of inorganic phases increases abruptly first and then decreases rapidly. It proves a bilayer structure with a surface organic layer and an internal inorganic layer, as illustrated in the 3D view (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408161-fig-0004">4b</a>). The SEI surface of the anode in the G2/DOL electrolyte shows a high content of both organic and inorganic species (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S37b</a>, Supporting Information). The C<sub>2</sub>HO<sup>–</sup> content gradually decreases with sputtering, and the inorganic content rises slightly and then gradually decreases, showing an organic-inorganic hybrid SEI structure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408161-fig-0004">4c</a>). The THF electrolyte exhibits decreasing C<sub>2</sub>HO<sup>−</sup> content over sputtering with the inorganic content remaining low (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S38</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S39</a>, Supporting Information). It indicates an organic-dominated SEI structure that results in poor Na reversibility, which accords well with the above DFT simulations (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S40</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>The above-mentioned nanostructure of SEI was further validated by cryo-transmission electron microscopy (cryo-TEM). The MeTHF electrolyte-derived SEI shows a typical bilayer structure with a thin organic film (≈5&nbsp;nm) at the surface and a thick inorganic layer (≈30&nbsp;nm) in the interior (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202408161-fig-0005">5a,b</a>). The crystalline nature of the inorganic SEI component corresponds to Na<sub>2</sub>O, Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, and NaF through the lattice fringe analysis. The G2/DOL system-derived SEI exhibits a typical organic-inorganic hybrid structure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408161-fig-0005">5d,e</a>). To obtain more detailed information on the electronic states and distribution of SEI components, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) with in-depth Ar<sup>+</sup> sputtering was carried out. The surface of the SEI formed in the MeTHF electrolyte shows a strong C─C/C─H peak centered at 284.8&nbsp;eV in the fitted C 1s spectrum (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S41a</a>, Supporting Information) and a moderate-intensity Na─F peak at 684.27&nbsp;eV in the F 1s spectrum, which is reported to be ascribed to PO<sub>y</sub>F<sub>z</sub>-containing organic species (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S41b</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0035_R_d40189270e1583" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Electrochem. Soc. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> An abrupt decrease in the C─C/C─H peak intensity is observed after sputtering for 8 s, which then continuously decreases over prolonged sputtering. This observation confirms the existence of a thin organic layer at the surface. In contrast, the intensity of the Na─F peak gradually increases due to the appearance of the interior inorganic layer. As for the G2/DOL system, the intensities of the C signal and F signal decreased simultaneously (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S42</a>, Supporting Information), which is the typical characteristic of an organic-inorganic hybrid SEI structure. Based on the results of ToF-SIMS, cryo-TEM, and XPS testing, we conclude the formation of a bilayer SEI structure with a compact organic-rich exterior and inorganic Na<sub>2</sub>O/NaF-rich interior in the MeTHF electrolyte.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202408161-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b2a72ef9-2648-44af-97f7-7617b67d0680/adma202408161-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b2a72ef9-2648-44af-97f7-7617b67d0680/adma202408161-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fe005979-629d-4163-8e11-2dc33178c2c0/adma202408161-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b2a72ef9-2648-44af-97f7-7617b67d0680/adma202408161-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202408161-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202408161" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b,d,e) Physicochemical properties of SEI. Cryo-TEM images and the schematic illustrations of the SEI after cycling at −25&nbsp;°C for the MeTHF (a,b) and G2/DOL (d,e) electrolytes. Scale bars, 10&nbsp;nm. c,f) AFM surface profiling of SEI formed in the MeTHF (c) and G2/DOL (f) electrolytes at −25&nbsp;°C. g) Exchange-current densities (<i>j<sub>0</sub></i>) calculated from Tafel plots. h,i) Schematic illustration of the SEI structures formed in the MeTHF (h) and G2/DOL (i) electrolytes.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The reversibility of Na plating/stripping is highly correlated with the mechanical and Na<sup>+</sup>-diffusion properties of the SEI. To evaluate the mechanical properties of the as-produced SEI, atomic force microscopy (AFM) measurement was performed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408161-fig-0005">5c,f</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S43</a>, Supporting Information). The average Young's modulus of SEI formed in the MeTHF electrolyte was 10.8 and 6.2&nbsp;GPa at RT and −25&nbsp;°C, respectively, much higher than those of the G2/DOL counterpart (8.5 and 4.3&nbsp;GPa). Such mechanical strength enhancement is attributed to the rich NaF component in the inner layer, which owns a high Young's modulus of 76&nbsp;GPa.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0036_R_d40189270e1629" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It effectively suppresses the growth and penetration of dendrites, thereby promoting enhanced reversibility of the Na anode. Moreover, the plated Na metal in the MeTHF electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408161-fig-0005">5c</a>) displays a notably smoother morphology with a surface roughness of 37.6&nbsp;nm than the G2/DOL electrolyte with a surface roughness of 45.2&nbsp;nm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408161-fig-0005">5f</a>). The morphology of plated Na metal was also observed by scanning electron microscopy (SEM), which shows dense, uniform, and dendrite-free sodium deposits in the MeTHF electrolyte at both −25 and −40&nbsp;°C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S44a,b</a>, Supporting Information). In contrast, sharp cracks and uneven deposit pits appear in the G2/DOL system owing to the unstable SEI and “dead” Na formation even at −25&nbsp;°C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S44c,d</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>High interfacial exchange current density (<i>j</i><sub>0</sub>) is reported to be essential for achieving highly reversible Na plating/stripping.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408161-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408161-bib-0037_R_d40189270e1653" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Nat. Energy">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The <i>j</i><sub>0</sub> values of MeTHF and G2/DOL electrolytes were evaluated by linear fitting of Tafel plots at various temperatures (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S45</a>, Supporting Information). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408161-fig-0005">5g</a>, the G2/DOL system exhibits a high <i>j<sub>0</sub></i> value of 2.07&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> at 25&nbsp;°C but maintains only 0.12&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> at −40&nbsp;°C. The MeTHF electrolyte manifests a similar room-temperature <i>j<sub>0</sub></i> value of 1.53&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> and remains as high as 0.56&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> even at −40&nbsp;°C, thus affording sufficient Na<sup>+</sup> ions beneath SEI to participate in the interfacial redox reaction. Based on the theoretical calculations as well as structural and electrochemical measurements, we anticipate that the superior LT Na reversibility is ascribed to the small aggregated dominated electrolyte with highly efficient and LT-resistant ion-conducting channels and the derived composition-homogenous, high-strength, and highly Na<sup>+</sup>-conducting SEI (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408161-fig-0005">5h</a>). It is superior to the conventional SSIP-dominated electrolyte with LT-sensitive Na<sup>+</sup>-conduction pathway and derived organic-inorganic hybrid SEI (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408161-fig-0005">5i</a>).</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202408161-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202408161-sec-0070-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we have designed a MeTHF-based dilute electrolyte to achieve a superior ionic conductivity of 3.58&nbsp;mS&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> and beyond 99.9% Na plating/stripping Coulombic efficiency even at −40&nbsp;°C. The as-formulated electrolyte shows a weak dissociation of Na<sup>+</sup>-PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> ion pairs but highly efficient Na<sup>+</sup> conducting channels. The AGG/CIP (≈3:1 ratio) dominated solvation structure helps form an effective network for ultrafast Na<sup>+</sup> hopping. Intriguingly, such solvation structure is well preserved at LT and breaks through the intermolecular attraction-induced LT ion-conducting limitation of conventional electrolytes. The LT-resistant ion-conducting channels assure smooth charge carrier transfer at reduced temperatures. Moreover, the well-regulated electronic structure by the methyl functional group enables preferential anion-decomposition to form a favorable bilayer SEI consisting of an inorganic interior and an organic surface, revealed by theoretical simulations and ToF-SIMS, XPS, cryo-TEM, AFM characterizations. It affords uniform Na<sup>+</sup> conduction channels for even Na deposition and high mechanical strength to inhibit Na dendrite growth. The as-constructed AFSMB cells sustain 300 cycles with 80% capacity maintained. A 0.5-Ah level anode-free pouch cell delivers 85% of the first discharge capacity over 180 cycles at −25&nbsp;°C. Findings will be of immediate benefit for the design of long-life and high-performance AFSMBs at LT. The electrolyte design principles in this work can also be extended to other LT battery systems.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

      <section id="openResearch" class="article-section article-section__open-research">
         <div class="accordion article-accordion">
            <h2><a href="#" class="accordion__control" role="button" aria-controls="open-research-content" aria-expanded="false"><span class="section__title" id="open-research-section">Open Research</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a></h2>
            <div class="accordion__content" role="region" aria-labelledby="open-research-section" id="open-research-content">
